  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_9981b072-96e6-4159-bbed-97834b2be85d'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5823644710377720809">An ivy league murder, a mysterious coded manuscript, and the secrets of a Renaissance prince collide memorably in <i>The Rule of Four</i> -- a brilliant work of fiction that weaves together suspense and scholarship, high art and unimaginable treachery.<br/><br/>It's Easter at Princeton. Seniors are scrambling to finish their theses. And two students, Tom Sullivan and Paul Harris, are a hai</span>
  <span id="freeText5823644710377720809" style="display:none">An ivy league murder, a mysterious coded manuscript, and the secrets of a Renaissance prince collide memorably in <i>The Rule of Four</i> -- a brilliant work of fiction that weaves together suspense and scholarship, high art and unimaginable treachery.<br /><br />It's Easter at Princeton. Seniors are scrambling to finish their theses. And two students, Tom Sullivan and Paul Harris, are a hair's breadth from solving the mysteries of the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili--a renowned text attributed to an Italian nobleman, a work that has baffled scholars since its publication in 1499. For Tom, their research has been a link to his family's past -- and an obstacle to the woman he loves. For Paul, it has become an obsession, the very reason for living. But as their deadline looms, research has stalled -- until a long-lost diary surfaces with a vital clue. And when a fellow researcher is murdered just hours later, Tom and Paul realize that they are not the first to glimpse the Hypnerotomachia 's secrets.<br /><br />Suddenly the stakes are raised, and as the two friends sift through the codes and riddles at the heart of the text, they are beginnning to see the manuscript in a new light--not simply as a story of faith, eroticism and pedantry, but as a bizarre, coded mathematical maze. And as they come closer and closer to deciphering the final puzzle of a book that has shattered careers, friendships and families, they know that their own lives are in mortal danger. Because at least one person has been killed for knowing too much. And they know even more.<br /><br />From the streets of fifteenth-century Rome to the rarified realm of Princeton, from a shocking 500 year-old murder scene to the drama of a young man's coming of age, <i>The Rule of Four</i> takes us on an entertaining, illuminating tour of history--as it builds to a pinnacle of nearly unbearable suspense.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5823644710377720809" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=18431 >

    <div class="u-positionRelative">

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/197932-i-m-not-sure-i-can-finish-this-book-i-am">I&#39;m not sure I can finish this book: I am flabbergasted by the utter implausibility of Princeton&#39;s first snowfall of the school year happening in April. What?? I spent seven years at Princeton, in the 90s, when this supposedly occurred, and it was always snowing by November; December at the latest. Is it worth continuing, or will I just encounter more distractions like this?</a>
</div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Oh! I didn't know that. But I know how that feel. But I guess having never been at Princeton and experiencing snow. I did not gave much attention to t<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Oh! I didn't know that. But I know how that feel. But I guess having never been at Princeton and experiencing snow. I did not gave much attention to that. <br /><br />But since I got curious... the story started during 1999, 5 centuries after the original text was printed. I found articles at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.princeton.edu/pr/news/99/c/0421.htm">https://www.princeton.edu/pr/news/99/...</a>. <br /><br />The Press (Christchurch)<br />Copyright 1999 The Christchurch Press Company Limited<br />April 19, 1999<br />HEADLINE: DIARY<br />BYLINE: DUNBAR Anna<br /><br />Barely sporting<br /><br />THE Princeton UniversityBoard of Trustees in New Jersey has voted to ban the students' traditional "nude olympics" after this year's event turned into an alcoholic brawl. The Daily Princetonian newspaper reports the board had voted to abolish the 30-year-old annual ritual, in which students gather for a nude frolic at midnight after the year's first snowfall. After this year's event, students at the elite Ivy League school complained of being sexually groped, and more than a dozen of the 350 participants landed in hospitals with alcohol poisoning or injuries.<br /><br />Sunday Times (London)<br />Copyright 1999 Times Newspapers Limited<br />April 18, 1999, Sunday<br />HEADLINE: Sports story of the week<br /><br />The annual Nude Olympics at Princeton University, New Jersey, has been banned after this year's event turned into an alcoholic brawl. For the past 30 years, students have marked the first snowfall of the year by a naked midnight frolic. But this year 350 people were taken to hospital with alcoholic poisoning and others complained of groping.<br /><br />Hope that helped. :)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_36283848" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36283848" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36283848?book_show_action=true">Oct 26, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36283848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9712854448873280462">Princeton undergrads become obsessed with figuring out the riddles in a five hundred year old book, the Hypneratomachia. The obsession was not new with them. It had puzzled researchers for hundreds of year, in particular the parents of two of the students. The puzzling is interesting. The intrigue of battling scholars and murder on campus is tedious, and the description of campus life and the protagonist’s romantic entanglement are mostly annoying. I found myself practically skimming some passag</span>
  <span id="freeText9712854448873280462" style="display:none">Princeton undergrads become obsessed with figuring out the riddles in a five hundred year old book, the Hypneratomachia. The obsession was not new with them. It had puzzled researchers for hundreds of year, in particular the parents of two of the students. The puzzling is interesting. The intrigue of battling scholars and murder on campus is tedious, and the description of campus life and the protagonist’s romantic entanglement are mostly annoying. I found myself practically skimming some passages. I also never quite got the main buddies straight, and had trouble keeping them straight in my mind as I read. This contributed to a feeling of “who cares anyway?” Given that I often found myself reading at odd times, like while walking from one end of a subway platform to another, there must be something compelling about the book. There is much payload on ancient learning, and the info about Savonarola and the conflict in Florence between the humanist intellectuals and the church is very interesting, I hesitate to give this a full thumbs up. Let’s say I find the obvious youth of the authors a barrier, but there is enough there to sustain interest, and enough payload to make the trip worth while. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9712854448873280462" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2941504" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2941504" />
    <a title="Keith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/184158-keith"><img alt="Keith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403532993p2/184158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2941504?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keith" href="/user/show/184158-keith">Keith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who are nostalgic about their ivy league college days</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2941504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8045640979661552409">This book was billed as a more intellectual version of The Da Vinci Code, and while I suppose it is essentially that, I honestly did not enjoy it as much as I enjoyed Dan Brown's book. The story is about a Princeton student who inherits from his father an obsession with an ancient text called the Hypnerotomachia, purported to contain directions to a vault of treasure.<br/><br/>Unfortunately, less than half the book was really devoted to the treasure hunt itself, with the remainder consisting of too-extens</span>
  <span id="freeText8045640979661552409" style="display:none">This book was billed as a more intellectual version of The Da Vinci Code, and while I suppose it is essentially that, I honestly did not enjoy it as much as I enjoyed Dan Brown's book. The story is about a Princeton student who inherits from his father an obsession with an ancient text called the Hypnerotomachia, purported to contain directions to a vault of treasure.<br /><br />Unfortunately, less than half the book was really devoted to the treasure hunt itself, with the remainder consisting of too-extensive background stories about the main characters and the ways in which they grew apart as they got older. Don't get me wrong; this is a perfectly valid thing for a story to be about. I just personally wasn't nearly as interested in these fairly generic characters going through fairly generic experiences at college as I was in the deciphering of a mysterious text. And it's definitely not just any college; it's PRINCETON. The book reminds you of this left and right, to the extent that in the end you feel like half the reason this book exists is that the authors really wanted to brag about how great Princeton is and how great they are for having gone there.<br /><br />All in all, the book carried my through to the end, and the Da Vinci Code-like sequences in which the characters unraveled the text's mysteries were entertaining. Those were just too few and far between for my liking.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8045640979661552409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5721443" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5721443" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38116-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5721443?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/38116-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440296409" href="/book/show/871113.The_Rule_of_Four">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38116-emily?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5721443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6231133026386969435">A Mr. Nelson DeMille writes on the back of this book that, "If Scott Fitzgerald, Umberto Eco, and Dan Brown teamed up to write a novel, the result would be <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four' title='The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rule of Four</a>." I don't believe...I just can't...words fail me. F. Scott Fitzgerald must be spinning in his grave right now. Comparing Ian Caldwell and Dustin Thomason to Fitzgerald? Blasphemy. As for comparing them to Dan Brown, they're not even the poor man's Dan Brown - more like the homeless man's, if that. (I haven't read Umberto Eco,</span>
  <span id="freeText6231133026386969435" style="display:none">A Mr. Nelson DeMille writes on the back of this book that, "If Scott Fitzgerald, Umberto Eco, and Dan Brown teamed up to write a novel, the result would be <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four" title="The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rule of Four</a>." I don't believe...I just can't...words fail me. F. Scott Fitzgerald must be spinning in his grave right now. Comparing Ian Caldwell and Dustin Thomason to Fitzgerald? Blasphemy. As for comparing them to Dan Brown, they're not even the poor man's Dan Brown - more like the homeless man's, if that. (I haven't read Umberto Eco, so I can't comment on that.)<br /><br />This book was really one of the worst books I've read in awhile. I mean, I came into it with low expectations - I didn't get the chance to buy any books for my long flight back to Taiwan, so I had to make do with this being the only book available at my parents' house - but even with my low expectations, this book was still truly awful. The "mystery" (if you can even call it that) centers around solving the translation for a centuries old text. I put "mystery" in quotes because this was one of the most boring, utterly disengaging mysteries I've ever read. If you like to read stories about people sitting around in basements reading old dusty books, then perhaps you'll find it more exciting than I did. There's also an attempt at some sort of coming of age story, which fails miserably. <br /><br />I don't know why when I don't like a particular book, I still force myself to read it to the bitter end. In this case, I wish I just stopped, even if that meant I would be sitting on an airplane staring blankly into space for hours. That would have still been a more worthwhile way of spending my time, instead of reading this junk. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6231133026386969435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1252166817" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1252166817" />
    <a title="Sean Gibson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20955173-sean-gibson"><img alt="Sean Gibson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416866667p2/20955173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1252166817?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Gibson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Gibson" href="/user/show/20955173-sean-gibson">Sean Gibson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1252166817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5077975325145287518">It’s a pretty safe bet that a dude who writes books about mysterious books is probably going to dig a book about a mysterious book. Then again, not so long ago, it was a pretty safe bet that a crazy guy from a reality show wouldn’t have a prayer of winning a major political office…but, hey—California went and elected that Schwarzenegger guy.<br/><br/>(Not where you thought that one was going, was it? Settle down, everyone.)<br/><br/>Consequently, consider that I’m exactly the intended core audience for The Rule of </span>
  <span id="freeText5077975325145287518" style="display:none">It’s a pretty safe bet that a dude who writes books about mysterious books is probably going to dig a book about a mysterious book. Then again, not so long ago, it was a pretty safe bet that a crazy guy from a reality show wouldn’t have a prayer of winning a major political office…but, hey—California went and elected that Schwarzenegger guy.<br /><br />(Not where you thought that one was going, was it? Settle down, everyone.)<br /><br />Consequently, consider that I’m exactly the intended core audience for The Rule of Four when trying to determine whether you might want to pick it up.<br /><br />(Side note: on no less than three occasions when someone asked me what I was reading while I was in the middle of this, I called it “The Sign of Four,” which is pretty indicative of my general state of confusion, chronic lack of sleep, and Holmes obsession.)<br /><br />The Rule of Four is a well-crafted, slow burn of a thriller that will appeal to anyone who loves historical mysteries (particularly those that reference Renaissance Italy), likes academic action as much (or more) as physical action, and finds the close, relatively isolated environment of a college campus to be an ideal setting for a murder mystery-type scenario. My only real quibbles were the utter toothlessness of law enforcement officials (our intrepid heroes are picked up by the local constabulary on multiple occasions, only to face no real consequence which, given the circumstances of the story in each instance, strained the bounds of credulity in a way that the unlikely cracking of the hidden code in a five-century old book did not) and the occasionally awkward attempt to round out a character who, given the type of story, would have been perfectly fine operating in only two dimensions. <br /><br />(Okay, I had one other quibble as well, but it’s a relatively minor one: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[not revealing the full bounty our hero, Tom, would get to see when he and fellow problem solver Paul finally opened the crypt that revealed the mysteries of the Hypnerotomachia. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> That would be like not letting us see the full glory of the treasure Nicolas Cage and company found at the end of National Treasure, which would have been an affront to filmmaking. (Nicolas Cage, incidentally, is himself a national—nay, international—treasure, as is that movie, and if you disagree with that sentiment, you are wrong, reprehensible, and probably a heartless zombie with an attachment disorder; I’m sorry to be the first to tell you that.))<br /><br />Suffice it to say, this is a book well worth picking up if you, like me, are the kind of dude, dudette, or duderette (we’ll use that term to encompass all aspects of gender fluidity in betwixt the male/female ends of the spectrum) who digs academic mysteries and geekish, scholarly heroes. <br /><br />Right around 4.5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5077975325145287518" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13292135430091793264" style="display:none">I strongly, strongly disliked this book.<br />After I first finished reading it, I wondered if the reason I hated it was because it had been mismarketed as a Da Vinci Code analogue, and I do love me some sleuthing among historical artifacts. But no. <br />I hated it because I disliked the pretentious characters. I disliked the plot and the constant, preening, self-indulgent homage to the hallowed halls of Princeton. I am always thrilled to hear that people love their alma mater. Really. But I don't need a coming-of-age novel masquerading as a mystery to tell me about the fun to be had in Ivy League New Jersey. <br />Maybe I missed something. Maybe there actually was a gripping historical mystery that I could not see through all the orange and black. Maybe I will never care enough to know.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3251171-tim?shelf=good-firelighters">good-firelighters</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96730245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5587527668206883821"><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380333612ra/693368.gif' width='240' height='186' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Confession: This book was so dreadful that I was moved to create a new readometer especially for it. <br/>Another confession, I never finished this book, it is unbelievably dull. Sure I never got to the end, although several reviews suggest that there isn't really an end anyway but as far as I got it seems to be a pseudo intellectual group masturbation about the wonders of going to Princeton. Quoting as many classics as can be crammed into the storyline (there was a storyline wasn't there?). The whol</span>
  <span id="freeText5587527668206883821" style="display:none"><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380333612ra/693368.gif" width="240" height="186" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Confession: This book was so dreadful that I was moved to create a new readometer especially for it. <br>Another confession, I never finished this book, it is unbelievably dull. Sure I never got to the end, although several reviews suggest that there isn't really an end anyway but as far as I got it seems to be a pseudo intellectual group masturbation about the wonders of going to Princeton. Quoting as many classics as can be crammed into the storyline (there was a storyline wasn't there?). The whole thing seems to be filled with nonsensical name dropping of classical works, “look how clever we are that we have read this book or seen this artwork”. How on earth this book is a best seller I don't know. Perhaps you have to be a pretentious ex Princetonian to make it worth the read, maybe all the reminiscing about clubs and hallways, galleries and libraries will do it for you. For me I was expecting a story, some pace, a plot some eloquent prose.. you know a book, a novel, a story, entertainment. This was Enid Blyton’s secret seven dragged into adulthood without a plot . To bill this book as the "The thinking man's Da Vinci Code is an insult to just about everyone from Da Vinci to the thinking man and everyone in between. Painfully dull and a struggle to wade through, I rarely give up on a book but this time I have made an exception, utter boring crap and I have better things to do with my time than to wade through it any further. But congratulations are in order, this is the only book to date that I have rated as worthy of only a single star and that because there isn’t a minus on the scale.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1356964628249433932">This book is incredibly creative; I love how Ian takes the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili and creates a compelling story built around history and detective work. If you loved Dan Browns books because you where excited to figure out the clues and solve the mysteries then you will love this book. The story itself included just enough drama to not take away from the underlying teaching of the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili (I’m sorry, once I taught myself to say it once I can’t stop saying it now).   <span id="freeText1356964628249433932" style="display:none">This book is incredibly creative; I love how Ian takes the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili and creates a compelling story built around history and detective work. If you loved Dan Browns books because you where excited to figure out the clues and solve the mysteries then you will love this book. The story itself included just enough drama to not take away from the underlying teaching of the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili (I’m sorry, once I taught myself to say it once I can’t stop saying it now). This ancient book cloaked in mystery is really what makes this story so good, I found myself doing Google searches on it trying to see the pictures and looking for the clues and trying to follow it as the characters in the story followed it. Overall great book, one in which I walked away from it feeling like I understood a lot more about history and how the human condition affects it.</span>

<div id="review_2410297059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2410297059" />
    <a title="Syl Sabastian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48422533-syl-sabastian"><img alt="Syl Sabastian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520059100p2/48422533.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2410297059?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Syl Sabastian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Syl Sabastian" href="/user/show/48422533-syl-sabastian">Syl Sabastian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2410297059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16692785299142975605">There's a reason Steven King recommends never using a word if there is a simpler one that will do. Because, sadly, when authors stretch their readers, and those readers can't quite make the stretching, they end up feeling stupid. Tending to react badly to the experience. Or, overreacting, mostly with negativity. Unreasonably so. This book, from reading through its reviews after I read it, seems to do just that. <br/><br/>It's a good engaging read, well written. A detailed story, connecting to complexity a</span>
  <span id="freeText16692785299142975605" style="display:none">There's a reason Steven King recommends never using a word if there is a simpler one that will do. Because, sadly, when authors stretch their readers, and those readers can't quite make the stretching, they end up feeling stupid. Tending to react badly to the experience. Or, overreacting, mostly with negativity. Unreasonably so. This book, from reading through its reviews after I read it, seems to do just that. <br /><br />It's a good engaging read, well written. A detailed story, connecting to complexity and the non-usual. Definitely not going to be everyone's thing. However, personal like or dislike seems to be much confused with the quality of the work itself. I might not personally enjoy something and still be able to recognise and appreciate it as a work of good quality. Unfortunately, this does not seem to be common among reviewers, who assume if they did not like, then it "must" be bad. <br /><br />There is much to recommend the work, form its insight into Princeton University life to the illuminations on renaissance intellectual life. Most of all, it provides insight into Immersion and obtuse interest which can consume some. This might seem removed from our ordinary life, but, through the extremes portrayed in the book, we come to understand any mindset or psychology that can become waylaid by immersion. This can happen with ego, ambition, sex, career, status, or any number of "mundane" obsessions. <br /><br />This book does an excellent job of illustrating those diversive and sometimes destructive distortions of perspective which can result. Not to mention the power of choice, awareness and deliberation. The necessity of character, and how its lack is so consequential. If we look at the book, its story, the characters, from this more abstract perspective the story has much to offer indeed. Well worth the read. <br /><br />My rating standard is more strict than most, just how I started, and like to keep the consistency. This book would have earned a very high 4 stars from me except the ending I felt was weak relative to the rest of the book. It's as if the last part was written by other authors. Still good, but not as potent as the rest. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16692785299142975605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10155672649315057422" style="display:none">"The Da Vinci Code for people with brains." The Independent. <br /><br />Sigh. Yeah. More like a book for anyone who passed English 101 freshman year of college. At least the Da Vinci Code was a page-turner ... an idiotic and predictable page-turner, but still entertaining. In The Rule of Four, it takes 268 pages for two hours to pass. The male protagonists are four college guys who drink wine (yeah right) and watch Audrey Hepburn movies (suuuuuure), and one is such a genius that he can easily translate a 15th century Genovese dialect into English ... which can only be insulting to the reader "with brains" who knows that the Genovese dialect is a language of it's own, incomprehensible to native Italian speakers who live anywhere outside Genova. In the end, this boring story (which actually had great potential by exploring the origins of a mysterious book from 1499) is really just a pseudo-intellectual rag and a snobby history of Princeton University. Blaaaah. <br /><br />It gets two stars for a few interesting insights about love and life, and the one developed character who was a fascinating librarian who only surfaced for ten pages or so. <br /><br />Sucked. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12589281717841815194">Second time through; still one of my all-time favorites. The prose is elegant and witty, despite being billed in the "literary thriller" genre (think The Da Vinci Code). The characters are rich, deep, and believable, especially Tom Sullivan, the narrator, on whom I think I have a wee crush. His observations on the dangers of loving things that cannot love you back—in his case, books—have stayed with me since I first read this last summer. The Rule of Four reads like a memoir, a careful blend of </span>
  <span id="freeText12589281717841815194" style="display:none">Second time through; still one of my all-time favorites. The prose is elegant and witty, despite being billed in the "literary thriller" genre (think The Da Vinci Code). The characters are rich, deep, and believable, especially Tom Sullivan, the narrator, on whom I think I have a wee crush. His observations on the dangers of loving things that cannot love you back—in his case, books—have stayed with me since I first read this last summer. The Rule of Four reads like a memoir, a careful blend of wit and nostalgia and keen observations, with just the right amount of panache and hope thrown in for good measure. I can never put this book down; I am smitten, yes, again, beyond the telling of it.</span>

<div id="review_966730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/966730" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73865-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/966730?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/73865-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099451952" href="/book/show/18434.The_Rule_of_Four">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer966730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9560106994170330508">the short version: <br/><br/>As trashy as you'd expect, with the unfortunate surprise of being almost completely uninteresting. Also, the end of the book thinks it is romanticizing academics, but it's really insulting them. <br/><br/>the long version (what I wrote about the book when I first read it): <br/><br/>Another Ivy League Education Gone to Waste<br/><br/>There's a small selection of English language books in the lounge at work, and I picked up <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four' title='The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rule of Four</a> the other day. I recalled reading a -- mostly positive -- review of</span>
  <span id="freeText9560106994170330508" style="display:none">the short version: <br /><br />As trashy as you'd expect, with the unfortunate surprise of being almost completely uninteresting. Also, the end of the book thinks it is romanticizing academics, but it's really insulting them. <br /><br />the long version (what I wrote about the book when I first read it): <br /><br />Another Ivy League Education Gone to Waste<br /><br />There's a small selection of English language books in the lounge at work, and I picked up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four" title="The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rule of Four</a> the other day. I recalled reading a -- mostly positive -- review of the book when it came out, describing it as a Da Vinci Code-ish novel (although I haven't read The Da Vinci Code), an academic mystery / suspense set at Princeton University, written by two Princeton grads as a way to keep in touch after graduation (which I thought totally dorky, but maybe a little cute). <br /><br />You know those guys that you went to college with, who were maybe a little too articulate for their own good and who were super-excited to be at "X" institution and could enumerate the reasons why, but only in the most intellectually superficial way? (Yeah, douchebag, Schroedinger's cat is awesome.) Yeah, the book is written by those guys. I hate those guys. And while, clearly, people who I might not like if I knew them in person can write some really good stuff (for one, I'm pretty sure that Dave Eggers is the guy at the bar who won't shut up about 'how awesome would it be if we ...?'), these aren't those guys. <br /><br />The book is clubby, pretentious, exceptionalist, shallow, and romantic about life and love in the self-absorbed way that must seem very profound and gratifying for a couple of Princeton guys with not many female friends, but that no one over the age of 24 should be. In short, everything I always assumed about Princeton students but never had proof of. Before. Also, the mystery isn't very interesting.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9560106994170330508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2729200" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2729200" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/171980-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2729200?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/171980-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099451952" href="/book/show/18434.The_Rule_of_Four">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2729200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12904232843662671429">You can compare it to the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code' title='The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown' rel='nofollow noopener'>Da Vinci Code</a>, in that it has the same sort of genre. The difference is that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four' title='The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rule of Four</a> has more character development, and less thriller action. To me, the book seemed similar in pretense, but was smarter in the content. It had a scholarly feel, and not just a governmental action feel.<br/><br/>Beautiful analogies and allegories are utilized by the two writers to convey the character’s thoughts and musings. These were a pleasure to read and effectively added to the emotion</span>
  <span id="freeText12904232843662671429" style="display:none">You can compare it to the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code by Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">Da Vinci Code</a>, in that it has the same sort of genre. The difference is that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four" title="The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rule of Four</a> has more character development, and less thriller action. To me, the book seemed similar in pretense, but was smarter in the content. It had a scholarly feel, and not just a governmental action feel.<br /><br />Beautiful analogies and allegories are utilized by the two writers to convey the character’s thoughts and musings. These were a pleasure to read and effectively added to the emotional rapport that the book built with me as a it’s reader.<br /><br />Still, there are no high-speed chase scenes, no pentacles crafted from human blood, and no secret meetings held in the Louvre’s restrooms. This is not to say however that the book moved painfully slowly. Buildings did explode, and lives were lost, but most importantly, the excitement that the characters experienced when solving the next great riddle was translated and delivered right to the reader with utmost skill.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12904232843662671429" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Spencer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Spencer" href="/user/show/1297552-spencer">Spencer</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26274799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17019218905829010251">I don't even think this should have been published. This was the biggest waste of time. The book focuses on a manuscript (you never really learn how to pronounce it, even with the pronunciation guide) that has secret clues hidden in it that are uraveled by some friends. It's stupid. The plot is terrible and just as you think the climax of the book is coming up, it ends. This is one of the few books I tell everyone to steer clear of. Totally worthless.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2155787339" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2155787339" />
    <a title="Bonnie Shores" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25177308-bonnie-shores"><img alt="Bonnie Shores" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500403398p2/25177308.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2155787339?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bonnie Shores" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bonnie Shores" href="/user/show/25177308-bonnie-shores">Bonnie Shores</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25177308-bonnie-shores?shelf=other">other</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2155787339" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10918650832076737063">While I can't deny that The Rule of Four was well written, I also can't deny that it was a thirteen-hour esoteric, pretentious dissertation on two Princeton seniors obsessed with an ancient text called the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1440184110ra/15942157.gif' width='600' height='600' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>This book was a Da Vinci Code wannabe—only the "clues" seemed to appear out of thin air and you'd quite literally have to know <b>everything</b> to figure out the meaning of the obscure references. Instead of searching for and finding clues, Paul apparently had the brain cap</span>
  <span id="freeText10918650832076737063" style="display:none">While I can't deny that The Rule of Four was well written, I also can't deny that it was a thirteen-hour esoteric, pretentious dissertation on two Princeton seniors obsessed with an ancient text called the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1440184110ra/15942157.gif" width="600" height="600" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This book was a Da Vinci Code wannabe—only the "clues" seemed to appear out of thin air and you'd quite literally have to know <b>everything</b> to figure out the meaning of the obscure references. Instead of searching for and finding clues, Paul apparently had the brain capability of a theoretical physicist who merely had to think long and hard on something and <i>voilà</i> he figured it out!<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1508523055ra/24223975.gif" width="600" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The majority of the book was spent listening to Tom philosophize on his and his roommates privileged lives at Princeton and whine over his relationship with his deceased father. The murder that the summary refers to doesn't take place until the final quarter of the book and there is very little suspense. In my opinion, the summary was extremely misleading...<br><br><i>"The Rule of Four takes us on an entertaining, illuminating tour of history—as it builds to a pinnacle of nearly unbearable suspense."</i> No. Seriously. No. The "tour of history", which I would normally find interesting, was so convoluted that it was nearly impossible to follow. And lengthy "fact" recitation does not an "entertaining tour" make.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1508523779ra/24224071.gif" width="600" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The Rule of Four would have been much more entertaining if it simply focused on the four roommates—all completely different, but coexisting in functional relationships.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10918650832076737063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_39103311" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39103311" />
    <a title="Cyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1644412-cyn"><img alt="Cyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224655069p2/1644412.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39103311?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cyn" href="/user/show/1644412-cyn">Cyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1644412-cyn?shelf=craptastic">craptastic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39103311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1880404119699687818">Oh, "The Rule of Four." It's been a while since I read this, but I thought about it again when I saw not one, but <i>two</i> copies at my local thrift store yesterday. It came out in 2004.<br/><br/>Yes, it's that awesome. This treasure was published on the heels of <i>The DaVinci Code</i>- it was rushed out, and the editing and extremely poor writing style reflect this. Take one member of academia (Tom, a college student!), add a mysterious tome (the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili- it's got codes!), and all of the other peo</span>
  <span id="freeText1880404119699687818" style="display:none">Oh, "The Rule of Four." It's been a while since I read this, but I thought about it again when I saw not one, but <i>two</i> copies at my local thrift store yesterday. It came out in 2004.<br /><br />Yes, it's that awesome. This treasure was published on the heels of <i>The DaVinci Code</i>- it was rushed out, and the editing and extremely poor writing style reflect this. Take one member of academia (Tom, a college student!), add a mysterious tome (the Hypnerotomachia Poliphili- it's got codes!), and all of the other people who want to get their hands on this ancient text and have them travel to discover a secret historical mystery. It sounds a bit familiar, doesn't it? <br /><br />The books would have been interesting if it hadn't been for the name-dropping (it's set in Princeton, and they don't let you forget it), the fact that they explain each and every historical and literary reference to the reader, and having to wade through the story with four characters who you never really like. Seriously, I'm guessing these characters are the two writers and their friends, which is a shame because all four are ridiculously pretentious. <br /><br />Reading this book was a chore. There was potential, but not enough to excuse the terrible writing and blatant condescension towards the reader. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1880404119699687818" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_395879549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/395879549" />
    <a title="Kristy Carey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8092682-kristy-carey"><img alt="Kristy Carey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442982973p2/8092682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/395879549?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristy Carey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristy Carey" href="/user/show/8092682-kristy-carey">Kristy Carey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099451952" href="/book/show/18434.The_Rule_of_Four">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer395879549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer748518098932342761">Just before reading this book, I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6411961.The_Lost_Symbol__Robert_Langdon___3_' title='The Lost Symbol (Robert Langdon, #3) by Dan Brown' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Lost Symbol</a>. Before I reached the end, I'd researched the ending. While I sometimes do that with a movie that I don't really care about, this was the first time I'd done it with a book.<br/><br/>As I listened to The Rule of Four, I have several books on Audio that I listen to while driving, I was confused by the plot. While I knew that the <i>Hypnerotomachia Poliphili</i> was central to the story, the jumping back and forth in time was odd at times and I couldn't grasp w</span>
  <span id="freeText748518098932342761" style="display:none">Just before reading this book, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6411961.The_Lost_Symbol__Robert_Langdon___3_" title="The Lost Symbol (Robert Langdon, #3) by Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lost Symbol</a>. Before I reached the end, I'd researched the ending. While I sometimes do that with a movie that I don't really care about, this was the first time I'd done it with a book.<br /><br />As I listened to The Rule of Four, I have several books on Audio that I listen to while driving, I was confused by the plot. While I knew that the <i>Hypnerotomachia Poliphili</i> was central to the story, the jumping back and forth in time was odd at times and I couldn't grasp what I needed to care about this old book.<br /><br />Than I reached the end, I smiled not only at the actual ending, but in understanding.<br /><br />The confusion I felt was due to everything being shown from Tom's point of view, who spends almost all of the story being confused by his past and future colliding. So much of his life was filled with a love/hate relationship with the <i>Hypnerotomachia Poliphili</i>, that became melded with the death of his father AND a sense of both wanting to finish what his father had started, but not BE his father.<br /><br />As an adult who's lost a parent before 30 [older than Tom was, but in many ways still in a developmental stage], I understood the sense of being torn between who they wanted you to be and who you are...<br /><br />I was saddened when I finished and came over to write the review and saw so many negative reviews. This isn't in the same caliber as a classic, but in my eyes... Dan Brown could learn a thing or two on research from this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="748518098932342761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_279159354" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/279159354" />
    <a title="Kristel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4924229-kristel"><img alt="Kristel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302532626p2/4924229.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/279159354?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristel" href="/user/show/4924229-kristel">Kristel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4924229-kristel?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4924229-kristel?shelf=intellectual-thriller">intellectual-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4924229-kristel?shelf=thriller-suspense">thriller-suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4924229-kristel?shelf=read-2012">read-2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer279159354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10567050785974993716"><i>
  <a href='http://fanarchist.wordpress.com/2014/08/30/the-rule-of-four-by-ian-caldwell-and-dustin-thomason/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Originally posted on my blog.</a>
</i><br/><br/>Let's get something out of the way: <i>The Rule of Four</i> by Justin Thomason and Ian Caldwell is pretty much a paint-by-numbers affair as far as intellectual thrillers are concerned. There is, of course, an extremely obscure historical text called the <i>
  <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypnerotomachia_Poliphili' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hypnerotomachia Poliphili</a>
</i> that apparently has an arcane code within it, revealing an earth-shaking truth that may rewrite history. There is an obsessive soul, a senior in Princeton named Paul, who becomes so consumed</span>
  <span id="freeText10567050785974993716" style="display:none"><i>
  <a href="http://fanarchist.wordpress.com/2014/08/30/the-rule-of-four-by-ian-caldwell-and-dustin-thomason/" rel="nofollow noopener">Originally posted on my blog.</a>
</i><br /><br />Let's get something out of the way: <i>The Rule of Four</i> by Justin Thomason and Ian Caldwell is pretty much a paint-by-numbers affair as far as intellectual thrillers are concerned. There is, of course, an extremely obscure historical text called the <i>
  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypnerotomachia_Poliphili" rel="nofollow noopener">Hypnerotomachia Poliphili</a>
</i> that apparently has an arcane code within it, revealing an earth-shaking truth that may rewrite history. There is an obsessive soul, a senior in Princeton named Paul, who becomes so consumed by the mystery that he pushes away the people who love him in his pursuit of it. There is a narrator named Tom who has already watched is his father be consumed by the <i>Hypnerotomachia</i> until his death and is now watching helplessly as the same thing happens to his best friend.<br /><br />There are also deaths, because people who write their thesis on 15th Century Italian manuscripts live life on the edge.<br /><br />But for some reason, reading this book pushed so many pleasure centers in my brain in ways that made me forgive the banal writing and even the weird tonal shifts that it takes. When the story is not straining to be suspenseful or shocking, I actually found it kind of comforting. The hermetic setting of the Princeton campus may also have contributed to that, because it evoked associations of <i>Dead Poets' Society</i>, <i>The Gilmore Girls</i>, and other pop culture things about idyllic schools and youth.<br /><br />Also woven into the narrative is the theme of father-son relationships. Within the rarefied confines of academia, both Tom and Paul are ultimately seeking validation from father figures that seem to only convey their affection as it is related to history. I'm all about tender masculine relationships so those parts were really up my alley.<br /><br />The authorial decision to structure the novel as a thriller, I think, ultimately hurt the story. Had Caldwell and Thomason emphasized the coming-of-age and nerdy mystery aspects while softening the mortal peril, it could have been a more satisfying read. It's in books like these that you can really detect the bald commerce of the book publishing industry. <i>The Rule of Four</i> clearly earned a lot of money my attaching its name onto Dan Brown's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code" rel="nofollow noopener">The Da Vinci Code</a>
</i> (published one year before) but it also suffered when it comes to cultural esteem because of it. If it had been edited and marketed as, say, Carlos Ruiz Zafon's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shadow of the Wind</a>
</i>, it could have attracted the kind of readers who are interested in atmosphere and academic scholarship, rather than readers looking for zippy thrillers with Vatican conspiracies.<br /><br />I guess I like the idea of it more than its reality, which happens often enough. <i>The Rule of Four</i> has acute things to say about the futility and nobility of scholarship which really hit home for me and my own college experience. During those short years, you are put into this very unnatural environment where a missed term paper feels like the end of your life. It's a time when all the learning opportunities are there for the taking and you have all the time in the world to pursue all that you want to know. But of course, youth is wasted on the young.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10567050785974993716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_68361355" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68361355" />
    <a title="Katie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/661424-katie"><img alt="Katie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218590857p2/661424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68361355?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie" href="/user/show/661424-katie">Katie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6583181-the-rule-of-four">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/661424-katie?shelf=own-it-and-read-it">own-it-and-read-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/661424-katie?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/661424-katie?shelf=new-in-2009">new-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68361355" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4647863199389848898">The comparisons to The DaVinci Code are inevitable, and the substandard copyediting seems to indicate that The Rule of Four was rushed out in order to capitalize on the Dan Brown furor. That the mistakes weren't fixed for the paperback edition is rather puzzling. The reviews do seem overenthusiastic, though it figures that the New York Times would seize on this more erudite text given the opportunity to steer readers from Dan Brown. Overall, this book was less thriller, more bildungsroman, and I</span>
  <span id="freeText4647863199389848898" style="display:none">The comparisons to The DaVinci Code are inevitable, and the substandard copyediting seems to indicate that The Rule of Four was rushed out in order to capitalize on the Dan Brown furor. That the mistakes weren't fixed for the paperback edition is rather puzzling. The reviews do seem overenthusiastic, though it figures that the New York Times would seize on this more erudite text given the opportunity to steer readers from Dan Brown. Overall, this book was less thriller, more bildungsroman, and I appreciated the character development that the authors included. I'm not much for a page-turning thriller, and as I get a bit older I leave less and less of my time for books like this one. That said, I did enjoy it, both the academic puzzle (which challenged me more than Dan Brown's silly apple riddle), and the image of college life. I got the impression that the authors were trying to marry the puzzle of the Hypnerotomachia with the uncertainty that comes with leaving academia behind (or choosing to pursue it as a career) and beginning "real life." I'm not entirely certain that the attempt was a successful one. But overall, it was fairly enjoyable. If you're a confirmed Dan Brown fan for the thrills, this one might leave you cold, because the mystery occupies a relatively small amount of the text. But as a profile of the way that the relationships between fathers and sons and friends and lovers intersect and develop and falter, and the interests that take our hearts and time away from our the people that matter to us, it's a worthwhile read. It's true, though, that for a novel that appears to celebrate academia, there is a real indictment of the system here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4647863199389848898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6112903" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6112903" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/346802-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190160486p2/346802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6112903?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/346802-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">thrill seekers left wanting by The daVinci Code.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6112903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6570585903438276687">After listening to me complain about the terrible writing in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code__Robert_Langdon___2_' title='The Da Vinci Code (Robert Langdon, #2) by Dan Brown' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Da Vinci Code</a>, my roommate recommended this book as a more satisfying read that blends art history with a murder mystery. I liked it (and greatly preferred the authors' style over that hack Dan Brown's), but I didn't love it. <br/><br/>Nearly as interesting as the book itself is the story of the two young authors, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11111.Ian_Caldwell' title='Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'> Ian Caldwell</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11110.Dustin_Thomason' title='Dustin Thomason' rel='nofollow noopener'> Dustin Thomason</a>, childhood friends and recent college graduates, who collaborated for years to write <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four' title='The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'> The Rul</a></span>
  <span id="freeText6570585903438276687" style="display:none">After listening to me complain about the terrible writing in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/968.The_Da_Vinci_Code__Robert_Langdon___2_" title="The Da Vinci Code (Robert Langdon, #2) by Dan Brown" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Da Vinci Code</a>, my roommate recommended this book as a more satisfying read that blends art history with a murder mystery. I liked it (and greatly preferred the authors' style over that hack Dan Brown's), but I didn't love it. <br /><br />Nearly as interesting as the book itself is the story of the two young authors, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11111.Ian_Caldwell" title="Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener"> Ian Caldwell</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11110.Dustin_Thomason" title="Dustin Thomason" rel="nofollow noopener"> Dustin Thomason</a>, childhood friends and recent college graduates, who collaborated for years to write <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four" title="The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Rule of Four</a> and endured several rejections before finding a publisher who took a shine to their manuscript. In a <i>Washington Post</i> interview from May 2004, Thomason said "I don't know what it would be like to write alone." I rely on editors and proofreaders to help me shape my thoughts into prose that conveys its intended message, but I can't imagine dividing the nitty gritty task of writing with a partner. I stand in awe of teams who can share the spotlight graciously and trust in each other's talents. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6570585903438276687" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4054095143674840516" style="display:none">I am becoming more and more baffled as to what it takes to become a New York Times Bestseller. But basically:<br /><br />If you think books have too much show and not enough tell, if you're looking for a book with pages and pages of inconsequential back story, and most certainly, if you want to see how info dumping can be transformed into a art form, then by all means read this book!<br /><br />I mean, I'm glad two childhood best friends went to college and used their collective degrees to write a book together, but so much of this book was unnecessary to read (there's a reason it's called back story.           <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1876124-bonnie?shelf=thrill-me-chill-me">thrill-me-chill-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1876124-bonnie?shelf=schlock">schlock</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90807916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14293919792646324909">Egads, this was BAD. If you want a love-note to Princeton, then read this. If you want something to actually enjoy, do not read this. I will admit that it captures undergraduate life, and especially senior year, pretty well. But it also has the world's most boring mystery and characters I didn't care two whits about and an ending I saw coming since the beginning. Just because a book uses big words, doesn't make it clever. <br/><br/>My biggest problem was the protagonist's girlfriend (I can't be bothered t</span>
  <span id="freeText14293919792646324909" style="display:none">Egads, this was BAD. If you want a love-note to Princeton, then read this. If you want something to actually enjoy, do not read this. I will admit that it captures undergraduate life, and especially senior year, pretty well. But it also has the world's most boring mystery and characters I didn't care two whits about and an ending I saw coming since the beginning. Just because a book uses big words, doesn't make it clever. <br /><br />My biggest problem was the protagonist's girlfriend (I can't be bothered to remember their names). As all too often happens in bad books, she has no real personality and serves no real purpose except to be a love interset, someone for the hero to pine for/fight for/give up the book/whatever for. Will they stay together? Won't they? Why is she so darn petty? I don't care! <br /><br />Plus, it was pretentious and had completely unnecessary flashbacks to the narrator's childhood that were supposed to "illustrate" the theme/point/whatever of that chapter. Worst one ever: at camp, a young narrator and his friends modify the "make new friends/but keep the old" campfire song in a stupid grade-school way, turning it into something about ditching friends and wanting gold (we totally did this with the school song in grade school, and we thought we were devestatingly clever). The point of this trip down memory-lane? His mother, in all seriousness, sits him down to talk about how this behavior makes her worried that he does in fact value gold more than friendship and to make sure he understands that people are worth more than objects because oh my god young narrator might become the scholarly obssessive that his father is oh noes! I guess young narrator got all his brains from his father, or perhaps it indicates that lack of common sense runs in the family, because I can't believe a human being is so oblivious that she thinks modifying song lyrics is a sign that a child actually BELIEVES what he is now singing. More likely, it is in fact a badly written attempt to shoe-horn the chapter's theme into a flashback. All these flashbacks, which in fact just boil down to My-Father-Was-Obssessed-With-This-Book-And-It-May-Be-Killed-Him-I-Don't-Want-To-Be-Like-Him-Do-You-Get-It-Now?-Huh?-HUH? could've been cut out and made it a better (but probably still not a good) book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14293919792646324909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Jeff Richey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/135553-jeff-richey"><img alt="Jeff Richey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559055635p2/135553.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff Richey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff Richey" href="/user/show/135553-jeff-richey">Jeff Richey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385337113" href="/book/show/227465.The_Rule_of_Four">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Suspense Enthusiasts</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2832088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14060918276774930450">I enjoyed this book immensely. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four' title='The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Rule of Four</a> came out around the same time as when the Da Vinci Code was a big deal, and other authors were jumping on the suspense/ historical fiction bandwagon. This was one of those books on that bandwagon. That being said, this is still a good read. The setting is Princeton. Tom Sullivan and Paul Harris are friends that have ties to a 500 year-old Renaissance book they are researching. The research is followed by many surprises, clues, solutions, relationsh</span>
  <span id="freeText14060918276774930450" style="display:none">I enjoyed this book immensely. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18431.The_Rule_of_Four" title="The Rule of Four by Ian Caldwell" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rule of Four</a> came out around the same time as when the Da Vinci Code was a big deal, and other authors were jumping on the suspense/ historical fiction bandwagon. This was one of those books on that bandwagon. That being said, this is still a good read. The setting is Princeton. Tom Sullivan and Paul Harris are friends that have ties to a 500 year-old Renaissance book they are researching. The research is followed by many surprises, clues, solutions, relationship conflicts, stress, and murder. I couldnt put it down, and it was a lot of fun, and becomes an obsession. There are many twists and turns followed by a goog wrap-up at the end of the story. Calwell and Thomason are both young authors with a great future of storytelling ahead of them. Rule of Four rocks! </span>
  <a data-text-id="14060918276774930450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11756691607067116252" style="display:none">Seemed like a pathetic attempt at ripping off Dan Brown's success, which is nothing short of delusional. While I could see why someone would want to recreate that amount of success, I see no point whatsoever in writing something as shitty as he did in order to achieve that goal.<br /><br />Fortunately, this book is better than The Da Vinci Code.
